  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer4985577150098690252"><i>The Largesse of the Sea Maiden </i>is the long-awaited new story collection from Denis Johnson. It follows the groundbreaking, highly acclaimed <i>Jesus’ Son</i>. Written in the same luminous prose, this collection finds Johnson in new territory, contemplating old age, mortality, the ghosts of the past, and the elusive and unexpected ways the mysteries of the universe assert themselv</span>
  <span id="freeText4985577150098690252" style="display:none"><i>The Largesse of the Sea Maiden </i>is the long-awaited new story collection from Denis Johnson. It follows the groundbreaking, highly acclaimed <i>Jesus’ Son</i>. Written in the same luminous prose, this collection finds Johnson in new territory, contemplating old age, mortality, the ghosts of the past, and the elusive and unexpected ways the mysteries of the universe assert themselves. Finished shortly before Johnson’s death in May 2017, this collection is the last word from a writer whose work will live on for many years to come.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4985577150098690252" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {

<div id="review_2188526279" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2188526279" />
    <a title="Angela M" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4685500-angela-m"><img alt="Angela M" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1512600770p2/4685500.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2188526279?book_show_action=true">Nov 18, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Angela M" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Angela M" href="/user/show/4685500-angela-m">Angela M</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/36280543-the-largesse-of-the-sea-maiden">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4685500-angela-m?shelf=netgalley-reviews">netgalley-reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2188526279" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2779803293502608725">The reason I wanted to read this collection is because of how much I enjoyed Denis Johnson’s Train Dreams . After finishing this collection of 5 stories, I initially rated it 3.5 stars feeling that some of the meaning had escaped me . But as I’m writing this and thinking more about it and the writing, I have to give it 4 stars. The writing is good and I liked three of the five stories so I’ll comment briefly on those . <br/><br/>My favorite is the first story titled as the book. Bill Whitman, an “ad man” </span>
  <span id="freeText2779803293502608725" style="display:none">The reason I wanted to read this collection is because of how much I enjoyed Denis Johnson’s Train Dreams . After finishing this collection of 5 stories, I initially rated it 3.5 stars feeling that some of the meaning had escaped me . But as I’m writing this and thinking more about it and the writing, I have to give it 4 stars. The writing is good and I liked three of the five stories so I’ll comment briefly on those . <br /><br />My favorite is the first story titled as the book. Bill Whitman, an “ad man” gives us a series of vignettes, depicting events and people in his life reflecting on marriage, divorce, death , careers. I especially enjoyed the discussion with a circle of friends who discuss the loudest sounds they remember or the most silent thing . This is not a typical conversation I could imagine being a part of but wow the responses were thought provoking and fascinating. A focus on mortality and as Whit puts it “the velocity of life.” (5 stars) The second story , “The Starlight on Idaho “is rather dark as we meet Mark Cassandra, “Cass”, in rehab for alcoholism and suffering side effects of the medicine, Antabuse. He writes letters to his childhood girlfriend, his AA sponsor, his father, grandmother, Satan, his sister, “friends and neighbors in the universe “, Rolling Stone and TV guide . It is though these letters that we come to know Cass , a good bit about his past and how he ended up here .(3.5 stars) “Triumph Over the Grave”, about a writer who talks about aging and illness, the death of friends, is depressing and realistic. It was eerie in a way as I read the last sentences: “It doesn’t matter. The world keeps turning. It’s plain to you that at the time I write this, I’m not dead. But maybe by the time you read it.” Dennis Johnson died in May of 2017. (4 stars) The other two stories I rate 3 stars. I just couldn’t connect with the Elvis obsessed poet . This may appeal to readers who really enjoy short fiction and fans of Johnson’s work. I own of copy of his Tree of Smoke, a National Book Award winner and hope to get to it one of these days soon. <br /><br /><br />I received an advanced copy of this book from Random House Publishing Group - Random House through NetGalley.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2779803293502608725" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2264130547" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2264130547" />
    <a title="Robin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36555254-robin"><img alt="Robin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536187488p2/36555254.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2264130547?book_show_action=true">Feb 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robin" href="/user/show/36555254-robin">Robin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=american">american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=2018">2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2264130547" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17800195393061613382">Not only had I never read Denis Johnson before this, but I'd never even heard his name before. How's that possible, to live my life, talking books with whoever is willing, not to know of this exceptional writer?<br/><br/>This collection is comprised of five sizeable short stories, written in a style that is conversational, meandering, unsentimental and poetic. These stories touch on the tricky business of living and dying, relationships, the absurdity, randomness and beauty of life, our unknowing and unkn</span>
  <span id="freeText17800195393061613382" style="display:none">Not only had I never read Denis Johnson before this, but I'd never even heard his name before. How's that possible, to live my life, talking books with whoever is willing, not to know of this exceptional writer?<br /><br />This collection is comprised of five sizeable short stories, written in a style that is conversational, meandering, unsentimental and poetic. These stories touch on the tricky business of living and dying, relationships, the absurdity, randomness and beauty of life, our unknowing and unknowable selves, addiction, among other things. The stories are masculine. They are edgy. They are whimsical and philosophical. I won't go into plots. What I will say is how they made me <i>feel</i>.<br /><br />I felt, in few pages, like I knew these characters, like I was sitting across from them over coffee, and they were talking to ME. I can see up close: a filling, age spots on a restless hand, a stained shirt cuff, a pink cheek at an emotional moment. They're telling me everything that is important to them, their pivotal life experiences. I'm there, with them, just me and them, as their stories unfold. What an intimacy.<br /><br />At the end of <i>Triumph Over the Grave</i>, it was even more than that. I felt it wasn't just a character talking to me, it was Denis Johnson himself, this extraordinary writer who died last year. Realising it was him all along, I was overcome with the beauty of it all. I was sobbing, I was undone. I know! Crazy, right? This is not a common occurrence in my reading life, let me tell you.<br /><br />What a powerful parting gift from Denis Johnson. While the final story about the Elvis obsessed poet didn't have the same allure for me as the other four stories, I cannot give this collection less than five stars.<br /><br /><i>I should mention I listened to this on audiobook - and it was excellent, narrated by a group of fantastic actors (Nick Offerman, Michael Shannon, Dermot Mulroney, Will Patton and Liev Schreiber). Highly recommended.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="17800195393061613382" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2304190240" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2304190240" />
    <a title="Larry H" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5872506-larry-h"><img alt="Larry H" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577826084p2/5872506.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2304190240?book_show_action=true">Feb 22, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Larry H" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Larry H" href="/user/show/5872506-larry-h">Larry H</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2304190240" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer238172608586793293">I'm between 3 and 3.5 stars.<br/><br/>"It doesn't matter. The world keeps turning. It's plain to you that at the time I write this, I'm not dead. But maybe by the time you read it."<br/><br/>Denis Johnson's last short story collection, <b>
  <i>The Largesse of the Sea Maiden</i>
</b>, was published about eight months after he died from lung cancer at the age of 67. That fact certainly adds a feeling of melancholy to the collection, even when he isn't writing lines like the ones above. It's also a fairly dark book about facing mo</span>
  <span id="freeText238172608586793293" style="display:none">I'm between 3 and 3.5 stars.<br /><br />"It doesn't matter. The world keeps turning. It's plain to you that at the time I write this, I'm not dead. But maybe by the time you read it."<br /><br />Denis Johnson's last short story collection, <b>
  <i>The Largesse of the Sea Maiden</i>
</b>, was published about eight months after he died from lung cancer at the age of 67. That fact certainly adds a feeling of melancholy to the collection, even when he isn't writing lines like the ones above. It's also a fairly dark book about facing mortality and one's failures. <br /><br />I first came upon Johnson's writing in the mid-1990s when I read his collection <b>
  <i>Jesus' Son</i>
</b> (way back in the days before I wrote book reviews or counted how many books I read), and it has honestly stuck with me all these years later. I forget at times what a phantasmagorical ride he often took you on, and that his stories had such surprising depth, even when they were a little bizarre, but his deft hand with imagery and word choice often had me re-reading paragraphs more than once, simply to marvel at what he had written.<br /><br />It was certainly inevitable that I'd come to <b>
  <i>The Largesse of the Sea Maiden</i>
</b> with higher expectations than I probably should have had, given these stories were the last thing he had written (at least as well as we're aware). Unfortunately, I found the collection somewhat uneven—a few stories didn't quite work for me, but they were bookended by one spectacular story and one really good one. <br /><br />I liked the story "Strangler Bob," a quirky story about a man in prison. While it, too, has some dark elements, there is more humor in this story than most of the others. But my two favorites in the collection were "Doppelgänger, Poltergeist," in which a writing instructor looked back on his relationship with his most gifted student, who became a famed poet, but who also had a strange obsession with Elvis Presley, and the exceptional, unforgettable title story, in which an aging ad man reflects on his life, his successes and his failures through the years, and some of the more interesting people and situations he encountered.<br /><br />In that story, Johnson shares some truly poignant lines which make it more evident he knew this was his final book. "I note that I've lived longer in the past, now, than I can expect to live in the future. I have more to remember than I have to look forward to. Memory fades, not much of the past stays, and I wouldn't mind forgetting a lot more of it."<br /><br />The literary world has lost a true treasure in Johnson, and if offbeat, beautifully written fiction appeals to you, I'd encourage you to pick up <b>
  <i>Jesus' Son</i>
</b> and <b>
  <i>Train Dreams</i>
</b>, his more recent novella. Those of you who are short story fans might enjoy this collection as well, if only for a few of the stories, but some may find it difficult to follow.<br /><br />RIP, Mr. Johnson, and thanks for sharing your immense talent with the world.<br /><br />See all of my reviews at <a href="http://itseithersadnessoreuphoria.blogspot.com" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">itseithersadnessoreuphoria.blogspot.com</a>, or check out my list of the best books I read in 2017 at <a href="https://itseithersadnessoreuphoria.blogspot.com/2018/01/the-best-books-i-read-in-2017.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">https://itseithersadnessoreuphoria.blogspot.com/2018/01/the-best-books-i-read-in-2017.html</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="238172608586793293" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2201547562" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2201547562" />
    <a title="Debbie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8665845-debbie"><img alt="Debbie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1334166735p2/8665845.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2201547562?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Debbie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Debbie" href="/user/show/8665845-debbie">Debbie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8665845-debbie?shelf=favorite-short-stories">favorite-short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8665845-debbie?shelf=netgalley">netgalley</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8665845-debbie?shelf=down-and-outers">down-and-outers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8665845-debbie?shelf=runners-up-2012-through-2019">runners-up-2012-through-2019</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2201547562" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9326867260526306745"><b>What is the loudest thing you’ve ever heard?</b> What about the quietest? This collection of short stories starts with a party scene where people are wracking their brains for memories. I want to be at this party! For a second, I forget about the book as I race down memory lane, wondering what I would come up with if someone asked me these questions. Seriously, these are delicious things to ponder! Already this writer has me in the palm of his hand.<br/><br/>Funny, I was dreading this book. First off, what’s </span>
  <span id="freeText9326867260526306745" style="display:none"><b>What is the loudest thing you’ve ever heard?</b> What about the quietest? This collection of short stories starts with a party scene where people are wracking their brains for memories. I want to be at this party! For a second, I forget about the book as I race down memory lane, wondering what I would come up with if someone asked me these questions. Seriously, these are delicious things to ponder! Already this writer has me in the palm of his hand.<br /><br />Funny, I was dreading this book. First off, what’s with the snooty academic title? I confess I didn’t even know what “largesse” meant—all I could hear was “large” (which is NOT its synonym, it turns out). And sea maidens--oh no! Are these stories going to be about myths or fairy tales, two of my most unfavorite things?<br /><br />And then there’s the blah cover. I like purple, I really do, but this cover is plain Jane, and the title doesn’t stand out enough. It’s in all caps, which I hate. And there are lines running through the title—in my editor eyes, lines through text mean throw the words the hell out. Even though I have the Kindle version and don’t have to look at the book, I know it has an ugly cover and lol, this made me not want to read it! “You can’t fool me, cover! I know what you really look like!” I figured anyone who picked out that blah cover must be peddling a blah book. Plus after reading a few reviews, I worried that this was going to an academic read, that it was Fine Literature full of symbolism and imagery, and I’d feel all depressed because it bored me or went right over my head.<br /><br />I couldn’t have been more wrong. You start reading and wham! You’re pulled right into that party scene with the two killer questions. But that scene, as rich as it is, is isolated, disconnected from the story. In fact, all five stories have the meander disease. But what I got instead of clean, clear plots were these brilliant little vignettes embedded everywhere, and I just savored them. There are some long-ass sentences, but who cares. I couldn’t take my eyes off his prose.<br /> <br />Each story is narrated by a man who is looking back at his life. There’s addiction, obsession, guilt, estrangement, worry, death. The author has a brilliant sense of the absurd, which is his ticket to my heart.<br /> <br />I ran into sentences like this, which made me swoon:<br /><br /><i>“How often will you witness a woman kissing an amputation?”</i><br /><br />The author died of liver cancer in 2017, and this was his last work. I’m thinking he was beating the clock and maybe he just didn’t have time to go for cohesion.<br /> <br />Death is a theme throughout the stories, though the collection isn’t maudlin. It’s clear that sometimes the author is looking at his own life, and there are pieces of him in his characters:<br /><br /><i>It’s plain to you that at the time I write this, I’m not dead. But maybe by the time you read it.”</i><br /><br />I decided early on that it was okay if plot wasn’t Johnson’s thing. I was mesmerized by his description of complex, down-and-outer characters, by his amazing insight into the human condition. And he’s one of those authors who observes the little things that we want to pretend we didn’t see or feel. Or maybe it’s not that we’re pretending events didn’t happen, maybe it’s just that these little things in our consciousness get trumped by the bigger things. Johnson brings the little things back to life.<br /><br />As always, I liked some stories better than others. My favorite was “The Starlight on Idaho,” about a guy in rehab writing letters to everyone he knows. I’m always a sucker for letters, anyway, and here I was enraptured.<br /><br />There was a little work required as I was reading. Whenever I opened the book, I had to reread quite a few pages and concentrate pretty hard. I think this is because the stories don’t have an obvious plot, and without an obvious plot it’s easy to lose your bearings. A minor complaint, however. The language is so rich, I didn’t mind the reread. I did run into the dreaded “try and” crime now and then, and I’d wince, but it was a one-off event.<br /> <br />You won’t find a lot of closure, but then again stories short on plot don’t necessarily warrant closure. The exception is the last story, about a guy obsessed with Elvis. That one had a tight ending even though the inside did some meandering. I wasn’t crazy about the story at first, but it grew on me.<br /><br />And weird—there are no chicks, anywhere. Don’t ask me why I noticed this! Occasionally a wife or a divorce is mentioned, but that’s the only inkling that another gender exists in the universe. That strikes me as odd, until I remember that we’re supposed to write what we know. Johnson knows men. He knows how to talk about what goes on in a man’s head. Thankfully, although the book is masculine, there is no macho.<br /><br />I got all busy checking out the author on the Internet, which I usually do when I love a book. I found out that he created this exquisite list:<br /><br /><b>Three Rules to Write By</b><br />-Write naked. That means to write what you would never say.<br />-Write in blood. As if ink is so precious you can’t waste it.<br />-Write in exile, as if you are never going to get home again, and you have to call back every detail.<br /><br />I’m not sure I “got” all the meaning in these stories—was there more to the meandering than I could understand? Dunno. But in any case, I was seduced by the polished prose and the intriguing, offbeat characters.<br /><br />So where has this writer been all my life? I must read his earlier works. What a shame that the fiction world lost this master storyteller.<br /><br />Thanks to NetGalley for the advance copy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9326867260526306745" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2244597585" />
    <a title="Diane S ☔" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4159922-diane-s"><img alt="Diane S ☔" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598402138p2/4159922.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2244597585?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane S ☔" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane S ☔" href="/user/show/4159922-diane-s">Diane S ☔</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2244597585" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7177723131107004063">Another literary icon has passed, leaving us with this his offering. Five stories, each longer than your usual shorts. The first, the title story, concern a man who works in advertising, he is nearing retirement, and he tells us in short vignettes about his dead or disappeared acquaintances. <br/><br/>All these stories grapple with death in all its different permutations. They oftentimes feature lives that have lost their way, their control of their future. My favorite was triumph over the grave. Where a </span>
  <span id="freeText7177723131107004063" style="display:none">Another literary icon has passed, leaving us with this his offering. Five stories, each longer than your usual shorts. The first, the title story, concern a man who works in advertising, he is nearing retirement, and he tells us in short vignettes about his dead or disappeared acquaintances. <br /><br />All these stories grapple with death in all its different permutations. They oftentimes feature lives that have lost their way, their control of their future. My favorite was triumph over the grave. Where a once successful author finds success doesn't guarantee happiness. It is the most poignsnt story and the one that closest relates to the author. I had thought I would love the last story with a character that has an obsession with Elvis, as I had an older cousin who was Elvis crazy. Unfortunately it turned out to be the one I liked the least. <br /><br />This is a strong collection, in my opinion, the stories tell it like it is, sometimes brutally. Glimpses of lives, in all their dark truths. He will be missed.<br /><br />ARC from Netgalley.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2409551002?book_show_action=true">Jun 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nat K" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nat K" href="/user/show/35251024-nat-k">Nat K</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/37904822-the-largesse-of-the-sea-maiden">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35251024-nat-k?shelf=2018-books">2018-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35251024-nat-k?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35251024-nat-k?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2409551002" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17512480750799589610"><i>”I note that I’ve lived longer in the past, now, than I can expect to live in the future. I have more to remember than I have to look forward to. Memory fades, not much of the past stays, and I wouldn’t mind forgetting a lot more of it.”</i><br/><br/>What an amazing platform GR is! Without it, I’d have been none the wiser, and would have been oblivious as to the existence of the writings of Denis Johnson. And what a shame that would’ve been.<br/><br/>There is something quietly haunting about these stories. Each one is </span>
  <span id="freeText17512480750799589610" style="display:none"><i>”I note that I’ve lived longer in the past, now, than I can expect to live in the future. I have more to remember than I have to look forward to. Memory fades, not much of the past stays, and I wouldn’t mind forgetting a lot more of it.”</i><br /><br />What an amazing platform GR is! Without it, I’d have been none the wiser, and would have been oblivious as to the existence of the writings of Denis Johnson. And what a shame that would’ve been.<br /><br />There is something quietly haunting about these stories. Each one is told from the perspective of one narrator. We’re taken on a journey with them, via their raw thoughts. We see the world as they do. Underlying pathos and angst, the characters are all going through emotional situations in their lives. We see the frailty of human life, both in body and mind. <br /><br />The pain of human existence, the humdrum of the day-to-day, the demons, the obsessions, the addictions. Somehow these stories capture the very essence of their characters’ lives. And it’s not always pretty. But that’s life too.<br /><br />There is beauty. In a snowflake falling to the pavement…<i>”Random snowflakes spiralled in the air.”</i> I could feel the serenity in the scene. I could see if slowly travelling to earth. Denis Johnson is a beautifully descriptive writer.<br /><br />There is also bitter and wicked humour scattered throughout. The story about the awards ceremony. The story about the AA participant writing letters to all and sundry, including the devil. The story of a young man’s obsession with Elvis and conspiracy theories, that continues well into adulthood. Life is stranger than fiction?<br /><br /><i>”Let’s just face the music and the facts. Somebody’s going out of my mind”.</i><br /><br />I’m a huge fan of the short story genre, and these five stories re-iterate why it’s such an amazing genre. If done well, it’s magic on the page. These stories are magic. <br /><br />While being hesitant to compare one Author to another, I can’t help but be reminded of Raymond Carver’s writing. Anyone familiar with Raymond Carver will know what I’m talking about. The layers of emotion contained in the stories, where as much as is being left unsaid, makes as much noise as that which has been. For a novella sized novel, these stories pack a depth of feeling into them. <br /><br />This is the sort of book that will leave me pondering for some time. It’s certainly left an impression.<br /> Loved.<br /><br /><i>Thanks to GR friend Cheri for talking about this book, otherwise I’d have missed out on something very special.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="17512480750799589610" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2382933430" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2382933430" />
    <a title="Cheri" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/106692-cheri"><img alt="Cheri" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601402708p2/106692.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2382933430?book_show_action=true">May 31, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheri" href="/user/show/106692-cheri">Cheri</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/36280543-the-largesse-of-the-sea-maiden">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=library-book">library-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2382933430" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10272639493672780383"><i>” Gravity is working against me<br/>And gravity wants to bring me down<br/><br/>“Oh, twice as much ain’t twice as good<br/>And can’t sustain like one half could <br/>It’s wanting more that’s gonna send me to my knees<br/><br/>“Just keep me where the light is<br/>Come on now, keep me where, now, keep me where the light is”</i><br/>	--<i>Gravity</i>, John Mayer, Songwriters: John Mayer<br/><br/><i>”It’s plain to you that at the time I write this, I’m not dead. But maybe by the time you read it.”</i><br/><br/>Not having read anything by Denis Johnson before this, and now having f</span>
  <span id="freeText10272639493672780383" style="display:none"><i>” Gravity is working against me<br />And gravity wants to bring me down<br /><br />“Oh, twice as much ain’t twice as good<br />And can’t sustain like one half could <br />It’s wanting more that’s gonna send me to my knees<br /><br />“Just keep me where the light is<br />Come on now, keep me where, now, keep me where the light is”</i><br />	--<i>Gravity</i>, John Mayer, Songwriters: John Mayer<br /><br /><i>”It’s plain to you that at the time I write this, I’m not dead. But maybe by the time you read it.”</i><br /><br />Not having read anything by Denis Johnson before this, and now having finished <i>
  <b>The Largesse of the Sea Maiden: Stories</b>
</i>, I am moved, but also sad that I have missed out on his books all these years before. It reminded me a bit of when I heard that Kent Haruf had passed, only a year or so after I’d discovered him. A gift I’d just been given had suddenly been given a limit. The second posthumously published book I’d read this week. <br /><br /><i>”This morning I was assailed by such sadness at the velocity of life—the distance I’ve traveled from my own youth, the persistence of the old regrets, the new regrets, the ability of failure to freshen itself in novel forms—that I almost crashed the car.”</i><br /><br />I’m not typically a fan of short stories, but these all seem related, if not connected by themes, which I love. Facing who we are, our transient nature, the elusive ways of love, and life, and all of the ‘unknowns’ of life. I felt a strong sense of being the silent person sitting by and listening, watching, these stories being shared, seeing the events unfurl, weighing in on these people living their rather ordinary, if occasionally peculiar, lives; their thoughts through which they condemn themselves and others, and seek clemency and mercy from the world. In other words, living. Life. Day by day by day. <br /><br /><i>”I note that I’ve lived longer in the past, now, than I can expect to live in the future. I have more to remember than I have to look forward to. Memory fades, not much of the past stays, and I wouldn’t mind forgetting a lot more of it.”</i><br /><br />I am choosing not to talk about the stories individually, but more about the themes that they collectively share, they are simply about life. Complex, messy, sometimes harsh, sometimes comical, even, perhaps, a sprinkling of the twisted and bizarre. <br /><br />I wasn’t drawn to this book because of the author as I knew nothing about him, and the cover / title certainly didn’t draw me to it. I was tempted by the reviews of my friends Angela and Diane, and with Debbie’s review I finally had to add this, and requested it from my library. I’m so glad I did. <br /><br />Please check out Angela, Diane and Debbie’s reviews:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2188526279">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2244597585">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2201547562">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br /><br />Many thanks, once again, to the Public Library system, and the many Librarians that manage, organize and keep it running, for the loan of this book!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10272639493672780383" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2259925178" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17186708653107106621"><b>Denis Johnson's Sirenic Stories, Visionary Tumulus at Sea</b><br/><br/>Once in a while, I know my lexicon is insufficient to give a book all due accolades. That, or I'm speechless from its hypnotic effect, or I'm worried I don't have time to write a review succinct enough that a potential reader will read it and be persuaded to read the book ASAP. Right now it's all of the above, so I borrow from others who've more experience and who were paid to review this Absolutely Brilliant book. Thus, below are the best</span>
  <span id="freeText17186708653107106621" style="display:none"><b>Denis Johnson's Sirenic Stories, Visionary Tumulus at Sea</b><br /><br />Once in a while, I know my lexicon is insufficient to give a book all due accolades. That, or I'm speechless from its hypnotic effect, or I'm worried I don't have time to write a review succinct enough that a potential reader will read it and be persuaded to read the book ASAP. Right now it's all of the above, so I borrow from others who've more experience and who were paid to review this Absolutely Brilliant book. Thus, below are the best and truest blurbs from reviews I read of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35135343.The_Largesse_of_the_Sea_Maiden" title="The Largesse of the Sea Maiden by Denis Johnson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Largesse of the Sea Maiden: Stories</a>:<br /><br />Sam Sacks, WSJ: “Johnson’s stories tread a crooked path through illness, addiction, criminality, mania and simple existential confusion. His gift is to extract the beauty in all that brokenness, like the painters who pulled holy light out of the wounds of martyrs. "<br /><br />Lincoln Michel, BOMB: "My god, that voice. Johnson somehow manages to be both conversational and poetic, simultaneously heartbreaking and hilarious... [with] an astonishing power to turn from one emotion to another in a line or two. His transitions between stories, sections, and paragraphs are worth the study of every aspiring fiction writer. [This is a] terrific book of heart, humanity, and humor. Read and treasure it. It is a final gift from a master."<br /><br />Maureen Corrigan, NPR: "Like those direct addresses to his future readers that Whitman scatters throughout Leaves of Grass, Johnson, in these stories, anticipates talking across the abyss that separates the quick from the dead... [The collection] affirms literature's promise to believers, the gift of eternal voice."<br /><br />Publishers Weekly: "a masterpiece of deep humanity and astonishing prose … an instant classic. It's filled with Johnson's unparalleled ability to inject humor, profundity, and beauty—often all three—into the dark and the mundane alike."<br /><br />Kevin Zambrano, The Rumpus: "[The characters in this collection] seem to see, as Wallace Stevens put it, 'Nothing that is not there and the nothing that is.' Perhaps this point of view comes from proximity to death."</span>

<div id="review_2120809280" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2120809280" />
    <a title="Angela M" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4685500-angela-m"><img alt="Angela M" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1512600770p2/4685500.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2120809280?book_show_action=true">Jan 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2120809280" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3928280956569271083">The reason I wanted to read this collection is because of how much I enjoyed Denis Johnson’s Train Dreams . After finishing this collection of 5 stories, I initially rated it 3.5 stars feeling that some of the meaning had escaped me . But as I’m writing this and thinking more about it and the writing, I have to give it 4 stars. The writing is good and I liked three of the five stories so I’ll comment briefly on those . <br/><br/>My favorite is the first story titled as the book. Bill Whitman, an “ad man” </span>
  <span id="freeText3928280956569271083" style="display:none">The reason I wanted to read this collection is because of how much I enjoyed Denis Johnson’s Train Dreams . After finishing this collection of 5 stories, I initially rated it 3.5 stars feeling that some of the meaning had escaped me . But as I’m writing this and thinking more about it and the writing, I have to give it 4 stars. The writing is good and I liked three of the five stories so I’ll comment briefly on those . <br /><br />My favorite is the first story titled as the book. Bill Whitman, an “ad man” gives us a series of vignettes, depicting events and people in his life reflecting on marriage, divorce, death , careers. I especially enjoyed the discussion with a circle of friends who discuss the loudest sounds they remember or the most silent thing . This is not a typical conversation I could imagine being a part of but wow the responses were thought provoking and fascinating. A focus on mortality and as Whit puts it “the velocity of life.” (5 stars) The second story , “The Starlight on Idaho “is rather dark as we meet Mark Cassandra, “Cass”, in rehab for alcoholism and suffering side effects of the medicine, Antabuse. He writes letters to his childhood girlfriend, his AA sponsor, his father, grandmother, Satan, his sister, “friends and neighbors in the universe “, Rolling Stone and TV guide . It is though these letters that we come to know Cass , a good bit about his past and how he ended up here .(3.5 stars) “Triumph Over the Grave”, about a writer who talks about aging and illness, the death of friends, is depressing and realistic. It was eerie in a way as I read the last sentences: “It doesn’t matter. The world keeps turning. It’s plain to you that at the time I write this, I’m not dead. But maybe by the time you read it.” Dennis Johnson died in May of 2017. (4 stars) The other two stories I rate 3 stars. I just couldn’t connect with the Elvis obsessed poet . This may appeal to readers who really enjoy short fiction and fans of Johnson’s work. I own of copy of his Tree of Smoke, a National Book Award winner and hope to get to it one of these days soon. <br /><br /><br />I received an advanced copy of this book from Random House Publishing Group - Random House through NetGalley.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3928280956569271083" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText9155636964779142757" style="display:none">I have been keen to read this posthumous collection of stories since my friend Neil nominated it for the Mookse group's best of 2018 poll, and it largely lived up to my high expectations - Johnson has a spare, distinctive narrative voice and his quirky stories are full of insight, humour and surprises.<br /><br />This book consists of 5 stories, all of which are between 20 and 55 pages in length. His narrators have a variety of backgrounds, but all have made mistakes they regret. The strangest is probably the final one Doppelganger, Poltergeist, which recounts the story of a poet obsessed with Elvis who pursues a bizarre conspiracy theory involving Elvis's substitution by his supposedly dead twin brother. <br /><br />I could say more about the other stories, but I wouldn't want to spoil them for anyone who has yet to read the book, which I recommend highly.</span>

<div id="review_2930541838" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2930541838" />
    <a title="Laysee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4863523-laysee"><img alt="Laysee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1459741824p2/4863523.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2930541838?book_show_action=true">Aug 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laysee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laysee" href="/user/show/4863523-laysee">Laysee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2930541838" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16587413186923803649">This week, I read one story a day from <b>The Largesse of the Sea Maiden</b>, as if I were on a lean reading diet. The truth is that the five stories can comfortably be read only one at a time because they weigh on the heart and some recovery time is needed before the next story hits home like a fresh thunderbolt.<br/><br/><b>The Largesse of the Sea Maiden</b> was Denis Johnson’s final work, published posthumously in January 2018. The protagonists in all the stories are desperate individuals given to dissipated lives l</span>
  <span id="freeText16587413186923803649" style="display:none">This week, I read one story a day from <b>The Largesse of the Sea Maiden</b>, as if I were on a lean reading diet. The truth is that the five stories can comfortably be read only one at a time because they weigh on the heart and some recovery time is needed before the next story hits home like a fresh thunderbolt.<br /><br /><b>The Largesse of the Sea Maiden</b> was Denis Johnson’s final work, published posthumously in January 2018. The protagonists in all the stories are desperate individuals given to dissipated lives lost to a combination of the following: alcoholism, substance abuse, mental illness, failed marriages, criminal activity, incarceration, and terminal illness. A fatalistic quality overtakes Johnson’s ‘criminally silly’ characters who spend their days “drinking, sinking’ in a perpetual hangover that never lifts. In his twenties, Johnson himself was addicted to drugs and alcohol, and I cannot help but think that he wrote this collection of short fiction from the depth of his suffering. In many ways, <b>The Largesse of the Sea Maiden</b> reminded me of <b>The Manual for Cleaning Women</b>, which drew heavily from Lucia Berlin’s years of bondage to alcohol. <br /><br />A preoccupation with terminal illness and death emerged as a theme in almost all the stories likely as Johnson was dying of liver cancer when they were written. His characters, like the writer-narrator in ‘Triumph Over the Grave’ literally watch death claim the lives of a colleague (Darcy Miller) and a personal friend (Link). What lends these stories their strength is this: <i>”They were the real thing, line after line of the real thing.”</i> (in the words of Professor Harrington, in ‘Doppelgänger. Poltergeist’, when paying tribute to the talent of a budding poet.) The characters tell their stories like it is. They almost always tell the truth, and if they do not, Johnson makes it known. The result is an honesty that feels uncomfortable but also tender and moving.<br /><br />Johnson wrote an incisive prose that was stark and immediate. No pretty writing to swoon over. Some passages evoked an atmosphere effectively: New York City on a snowy winter’s night in the titular story as the sleepless narrator went bar hopping or the thunderstorms and buzzards that heralded the deaths in ‘Triumph Over the Grave.’ <br /><br />When the last story was finished, I felt a sense of loss. This is the first work of Denis Johnson I have read but it was his last, and there will be no more literary contributions from him. Yet, he had evidently left a legacy of fine writing (e.g., <b>Tree of Smoke</b> which won the 2007 National Book Award for Fiction), which I intend to read in the near future.<br /><br />You may wish to stop reading here unless you don't mind a sneak preview of the five stories (which I've added to jolt my own memory in time to come.)<br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br /><u>The Largesse of the Sea Maiden</u><br />The story is notable for an after-dinner chat amongst acquaintances on the loudest sound and the most silent thing ever encountered. A range of raw emotions emerged for which words were inadequate. <br /><br /><u>The Starlight on Idaho</u><br />Mark Cassandra (“Cass”) wrote letters to everyone he knew (including Satan) about the 'hooks in his heart' and his sincerity about wanting to ‘stay in his lane and take it easy’ in an addiction recovery center. The letters became progressively more and more disturbing. Heartbreaking story.<br /><br /><u>Strangler Bob</u><br />The setting was a prison facility where a group of men, repeat offenders, awaited sentencing, parole, or death. This story read like an anthem for doomed youth for three very young men whose substance abuse had precipitated a string of criminal activities and time in prison.<br /><br /><u>Triumph Over The Grave</u><br />The story read like journal entries in which the narrator, a writer, was up close and personal with death as he cared for terminally ill friends and observed their bodies and minds disintegrating. Sadness is assuaged by some truly touching moments.<br /><br /><u>Doppelgänger. Poltergeist</u><br />The most bizarre story in this collection about a poet who had an obsession with Elvis Presley and a conspiracy theory. This is my least favorite story although the theme of the doppelgänger was explored very satisfactorily.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="16587413186923803649" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781787330634" href="/book/show/35657521-the-largesse-of-the-sea-maiden">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

<span id="freeTextContainer4394416258632029659">Had it not been for some GR friends raving about <i>The Largesse of the Sea Maiden</i>, I wouldn't have come across this short stories collection.<br/><br/>I'm also grateful to the public library system for purchasing this book.<br/><br/>First of all, I loved the writing.<br/><br/>As it's the case with most, if not all, short stories collections, I enjoyed some more than others. It's fair to say that the first three stories were my favourites - The Largesse of the Sea Maiden and The Starlight on Idaho and Strangler Bob. My enjoymen</span>
  <span id="freeText4394416258632029659" style="display:none">Had it not been for some GR friends raving about <i>The Largesse of the Sea Maiden</i>, I wouldn't have come across this short stories collection.<br /><br />I'm also grateful to the public library system for purchasing this book.<br /><br />First of all, I loved the writing.<br /><br />As it's the case with most, if not all, short stories collections, I enjoyed some more than others. It's fair to say that the first three stories were my favourites - The Largesse of the Sea Maiden and The Starlight on Idaho and Strangler Bob. My enjoyment dimmed somewhat for the last two stories. I still appreciated Johnson's writing skill, I just didn't find the stories as affecting or meaningful to me. I'm sure my disdain for all the Elvis apparitions and conspiracy theories had something to do with my lack of enthusiasm for the last story, Doppelganger, Poltergeist.<br /><br />The themes of these stories have a common thread as most of them are about ageing, disease, death, loneliness, regrets, substance abuse, obsession. <br /><br />I'm glad I've come across Dennis Johnson's writing. I now want to read more by him. </span>

<div id="review_2231743900" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2231743900" />
    <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy"><img alt="Jenny (Reading Envy)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455391853p2/68030.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2231743900?book_show_action=true">Dec 29, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenny (Reading Envy)" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy">Jenny (Reading Envy)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=reviewcopy">reviewcopy</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2231743900" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14149039140120772210">This posthumous story collection by Denis Johnson is my first time reading him, but it won't be the last. Most of the people who have more experience in the group where we are discussing this prefer collections like <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/608287.Jesus__Son' title='Jesus' Son by Denis Johnson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jesus' Son</a>, but I'll have to wait to weigh in on the comparison.<br/><br/>I chose the audio for this collection because of the narrators, so I will discuss both the story and the narrator. Overall, the stories are manly manly stories, but often about lessons learned, lives lived, endings and g</span>
  <span id="freeText14149039140120772210" style="display:none">This posthumous story collection by Denis Johnson is my first time reading him, but it won't be the last. Most of the people who have more experience in the group where we are discussing this prefer collections like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/608287.Jesus__Son" title="Jesus' Son by Denis Johnson" rel="nofollow noopener">Jesus' Son</a>, but I'll have to wait to weigh in on the comparison.<br /><br />I chose the audio for this collection because of the narrators, so I will discuss both the story and the narrator. Overall, the stories are manly manly stories, but often about lessons learned, lives lived, endings and ghosts. That should be no surprise since the author passed away before these were published. But I do wonder if all his other writing reads as masculine. It's practically Cormac McCarthy in here.<br /><br />"The Largesse of the Sea Maiden," read by Nick Offerman (text available online via <a href="&lt;br /&gt;http://bit.ly/2CVHdyV" rel="nofollow noopener">The New Yorker</a>)<br />Absolutely about an older man looking back at his life, navigating his present without always being 100% sure of his reality. Some painful-funny moments stand out, like not being sure which ex-wife is on the phone, or telling a co-worker's son to tell him hello even after they've had the conversation about his passing. Nick Offerman is always good, and a believable voice for this story.<br /><br />"The Starlight on Idaho," read by Michael Shannon (text available online via <a href="http://theharvardadvocate.com/article/164/the-starlight-on-idaho/" rel="nofollow noopener">The Harvard Advocate</a>)<br />This is my second favorite story, from the point of view of someone who has checked into "Starlight Addiction Recovery Center," told entirely through his letters to others. The audiobook narrator really brought this one to life, through the dry-miserable wit of Michael Shannon. It ranges in tone from sane reflection to batshit crazy conversations with Satan. I loved it. And the themes of isolation and regret start to feel thematic as they are present here as well.<br /><br />"Strangler Bob," read by Dermot Mulroney (text available from <a href="https://www.newyorker.com/magazine/2017/10/23/strangler-bob&quot;" rel="nofollow noopener">The New Yorker</a>)<br />Set in a jail, a man known as Strangler Bob proves particularly prophetic to a man serving a far shorter sentence. No real comment on the narration except I wasn't thinking about him, so he must have gotten out of the way.<br /><br />"Triumph Over the Grave," read by Will Patton<br />This is absolutely the best story of the collection, about an aging writer in the desert, but it veers into the magical or horrific or ghostly, depending on how you read it. The narration feels most strongly like it is the author, and with the way the story ends, I just don't understand how this wasn't the last story in the collection. Will Patton is excellent and I would be shocked if he wasn't somehow funneling the voice of Johnson.<br /><br />"Doppelgänger, Poltergeist" read by Liev Schreiber <br />Well, this was a strange one about a man obsessed with Elvis, also about the twin towers somehow. The weakest story, but I did like hearing Liev Schreiber as narrator, and I hope he does something else at some point.<br /><br /><i>I listened to this after its publication date (mid-January 2018) but through a copy provided by the publisher through the Volumes app.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="14149039140120772210" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2562276932" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2562276932" />
    <a title="Ron" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43881851-ron"><img alt="Ron" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557265090p2/43881851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2562276932?book_show_action=true">Oct 17, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ron" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ron" href="/user/show/43881851-ron">Ron</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43881851-ron?shelf=anthology-collection">anthology-collection</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43881851-ron?shelf=short-story">short-story</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2562276932" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10011027111647871987">Below I touch on the five short stories, which are anything but short in meaning or feeling. Two hundred pages can be read in a day, but who would want to rush these? Life is funny and also tragic. These stories can be both, equally.<br/><br/><u>
  <b>The Largesse of the Sea Maiden</b>
</u> - This first story consists of individually named short stories connected by a single character. It reads like snippets of jointed time, or periodic days, plucked from the memory of this one man's life. Each are very different, surp</span>
  <span id="freeText10011027111647871987" style="display:none">Below I touch on the five short stories, which are anything but short in meaning or feeling. Two hundred pages can be read in a day, but who would want to rush these? Life is funny and also tragic. These stories can be both, equally.<br /><br /><u>
  <b>The Largesse of the Sea Maiden</b>
</u> - This first story consists of individually named short stories connected by a single character. It reads like snippets of jointed time, or periodic days, plucked from the memory of this one man's life. Each are very different, surprising in their content, and completely absorbing.<br /><u>
  <b>The Starlight on Idaho</b>
</u> - Mark Cassandra, an alcoholic, pens letters of apology, regrets, complaints, etc. from Room 8 at the Starlight Addiction Recovery Center. Grandma takes a bashing, deservedly so, as well as Mom, Sis, Bro, the Doc, Satan and Antabuse. Very funny and poignantly serious in nearly the same moments. Loved it for that and because he doesn't forget himself in the mix.<br /><u>
  <b>Strangler Bob</b>
</u> - Looking back from some, or many, years down that road, “Dink” tells his tale of first-time county lockup at the unripe age of 18 with an unreal (and yet totally real) cast of characters he, nor I, could not possibly forget. Terrifically funny in wording and circumstance with an ending that feels like a cliff.<br /><u>
  <b>Triumph Over the Grave</b>
</u> - Unreal circumstances in the first moments here lead to the very real station of life. The unfolding of this story is unexpected, meaning I could not foresee the path of the plot, and did not care because I was completely involved. So good it feels biographical.<br /><u>
  <b>Doppelganger, Poltergeist</b>
</u> - The last of the five shorts is hard to describe within a sentence or two, so I'll just say it's about lifelong friendships, the familial bond of love that death does not break, and an obsession with Elvis. Denis Johnson integrates elements into this story that I, personally, would not imagine being together, and does it almost seamlessly. It meanders a bit, yeah, but comes home nicely.<br /><br />The fifth story, being my least favorite, says a lot about how much I <s>liked</s> <b>loved</b> the first four, because even the fifth is one I'll remember for some time. Johnson's writing is so good. I could count only a few writers as of late that could make me laugh, then ponder within only the span of a few short sentences.<br /><br /><i>And a big thank you to my friend Nat for this book recommendation.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="10011027111647871987" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

Johnson's writing style is so effortlessly poetic, improvised and almost experimental at times, yet perfectly orchestrated and genuine. Jazzy beats are always playing in the back of my mind as I read his words. The Largess of the Sea Maiden is Johnson at his best, mature and polished... Five memorable stories, each brilliantly narrated and put together. As a whole this collection is quite simply magical, a lovely farewell by a groundbreaking author.</span>

<div id="review_2209333995" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2209333995" />
    <a title="Neil" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33119635-neil"><img alt="Neil" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544045847p2/33119635.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2209333995?book_show_action=true">Feb 02, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Neil" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Neil" href="/user/show/33119635-neil">Neil</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/37904822-the-largesse-of-the-sea-maiden">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33119635-neil?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33119635-neil?shelf=2018-top-books">2018-top-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33119635-neil?shelf=5-stars">5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33119635-neil?shelf=re-read">re-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2209333995" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5601605604054559277">The thing is, if, like me, you have read nearly all of Johnson’s novels, there is something about his writing that means you feel like you know him. I know that’s not true and I know that the "him" I know probably isn’t Johnson in truth, but the honesty and rawness of what he writes makes you feel that way.<br/><br/>This means that reading a book of short stories published posthumously (he died in May 2017) turns into a very emotional experience. Especially when that book talks a lot about death. And even</span>
  <span id="freeText5601605604054559277" style="display:none">The thing is, if, like me, you have read nearly all of Johnson’s novels, there is something about his writing that means you feel like you know him. I know that’s not true and I know that the "him" I know probably isn’t Johnson in truth, but the honesty and rawness of what he writes makes you feel that way.<br /><br />This means that reading a book of short stories published posthumously (he died in May 2017) turns into a very emotional experience. Especially when that book talks a lot about death. And even more especially when the style and subject matter (and, occasionally, actual characters) of the book echoes what many consider to be Johnson’s masterpiece (and just about my favourite novel), Jesus’ Son.<br /><br />So don’t expect anything rational here. For large parts of the book, the text seemed to be a bit blurry for some reason.<br /><br />The Largesse… presents us with five short stories (roughly 40 pages each). The tone and subject matter make for a perfect follow on to Jesus’ Son. The first story gives the book its name and is a meditation on mortality as an advertising executive looks back on his life and recalls many of his friends and colleagues who are now dead. It is moving but surprisingly funny in several places. The Starlight On Idaho consists of unposted letters written by an alcoholic/drug addict from a rehab centre. The letters are written to relatives, doctors, the Pope and Satan. Again, there are funny moments but the overall story is a sad one. This made me laugh and I had to read it out loud to my wife:<br /><br /><i>"But just to catch you up. In the last five years I’ve been arrested about eight times, shot twice, not twice on one occasion, but once on two different occasions, etc etc and I think I got run over once but I don’t even remember it."</i><br /><br />In Strangler Bob we read about a man in prison with a cell mate called, unsurprisingly, Strangler Bob. Johnson was a poet before he was a novelist and this shows in a lot of his language (which is one of the things I love about his writing). For example<br /><br /><i>"He really was enormous, both muscular and overfed, looked fashioned from balloons, at least usually, but at this moment looked sculpted from quivering stone,…"</i><br /><br />Quivering stone!<br /><br />In Triumph Over The Grave we read about a writer falling prey to old age. This includes a quote that describes my feeling about Johnson’s novels even if I haven’t specifically done what he says:<br /><br /><i>"I looked back at the novel’s opening paragraph, and by midnight I’d read the whole thing again and found myself just as moved as I’d been the first time—the first dozen times—every time."</i><br /><br />This story also includes the quote the everyone is using in their reviews. I thought maybe I should avoid it because so many people have used it. But then, it is so apposite it is actually impossible to leave it out:<br /><br /><i>"The world keeps turning. It’s plain to you that at the time I write this, I’m not dead. But maybe by the time you read it."</i><br /><br />And then Doppelgänger, Poltergeist introduces us to a poet who believes Elvis Presley was killed early in his life (around the time he was drafted) and replaced by his twin (but less talented) brother who supposedly died at birth but was actually hidden away.<br /><br />I’ll finish with one of Johnson’s characters musing on writing. In its poetic way, it seems to sum things up.<br /><br /><i>"Writing. It’s easy work. The equipment isn’t expensive, and you can pursue this occupation anywhere. You make your own hours, mess around the house in your pajamas, listening to jazz recordings and sipping coffee while another day makes its escape. You don’t have to be high-functioning or even, for the most part, functioning at all. If I could drink liquor without being drunk all the time, I’d certainly drink enough to be drunk half the time, and production wouldn’t suffer. Bouts of poverty come along, anxiety, shocking debt, but nothing lasts forever. I’ve gone from rags to riches and back again, and more than once. Whatever happens to you, you put it on a page, work it into a shape, cast it in a light. It’s not much different, really, from filming a parade of clouds across the sky and calling it a movie—although it has to be admitted that the clouds can descend, take you up, carry you to all kinds of places, some of them terrible, and you don’t get back where you came from for years and years."</i><br /><br />RIP Denis Johnson. You will be missed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5601605604054559277" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
