<div id="review_2219325549" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2219325549" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1826682-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1252295186p2/1826682.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2219325549?book_show_action=true">Feb 11, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/1826682-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=america">america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=audio">audio</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2219325549" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15472459147316512193">I’d never read any Denis Johnson before this, though of course I knew of his work. I thought I had endless days to finally show my appreciation.<br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2267657859?book_show_action=false&from_review_page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>A GR friend </a>describes the eponymous first story as if watching a magician at work. That story is actually a set of very short stories, each so well-conceived and trimmed of fat that worlds are conveyed in a sentence. Perhaps he could have been a lyricist; <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2208261977?book_show_action=false&from_review_page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>another GR friend says </a>he was a poet “first and foremost.” Yes. <br/><br/>Both these GR friends cite that firs</span>
  <span id="freeText15472459147316512193" style="display:none">I’d never read any Denis Johnson before this, though of course I knew of his work. I thought I had endless days to finally show my appreciation.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2267657859?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">A GR friend </a>describes the eponymous first story as if watching a magician at work. That story is actually a set of very short stories, each so well-conceived and trimmed of fat that worlds are conveyed in a sentence. Perhaps he could have been a lyricist; <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2208261977?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">another GR friend says </a>he was a poet “first and foremost.” Yes. <br /><br />Both these GR friends cite that first story along with “Triumph Over Grave,” the penultimate story in this collection, as their favorites. So it is with me. These two stories are worth seeking out the collection to read. Probably you will not get a better idea of the art and the man than these.<blockquote> “What have you been doing with yourself out here?” one character asks another who is naked beneath his open lab coat. <br />“Thangdoodlin’,” the other man replies. —from “Triumph…”</blockquote><br />Johnson knew too much about addiction. It saturates his stories and while in some we get the sense of a understanding compassion for fellow sufferers, at the same time it gives us the claustrophobic I-can’t-breathe quality of hearing the same goddamn story again in its millionth iteration, perhaps even from the same person. Empathy, even sympathy, turns sour over too much time with addicts.<blockquote> “We alkies are just a tangle of lies like the insides of a golf ball.”</blockquote>At the same time I want to press this book into the hands of AA &amp; NA attendees to talk about at their choreographed meetings. Surely the vision of someone managing to describe their common symptoms and regrets is restitutive, reflexively imitative. But how would he know these things except to have succumbed more times than can be counted? Some folks manage to escape. We have to hold onto that. Besides, he never asks for more.<br /><br />Penguin Random House does a magnificent job on the audio of this collection, and provides some Soundcloud clips for stories read by different actors. <a href="https://soundcloud.com/penguin-audio/the-largesse-of-the-sea-4" rel="nofollow noopener">Nick Offerman reads from “Ad Man,”</a> one of the very short stories in “Largesse…” My favorite voice among all these favorite actors is <a href="https://soundcloud.com/penguin-audio/the-largesse-of-the-sea-3" rel="nofollow noopener">Michael Shannon reading the first page of “The Starlight on Idaho,”</a> sounding so much like Sam Shepard in voice and subject matter that we remember a time when these men roamed the earth. They meant something to us, addictions or not. <a href="https://soundcloud.com/penguin-audio/the-largesse-of-the-sea-1" rel="nofollow noopener"> Will Patton reads the first pages of “Triumph…”</a>, his voice all shaky and smoky like someone who will never lose the jitters anymore; the first page of <a href="https://soundcloud.com/penguin-audio/the-largesse-of-the-sea-2" rel="nofollow noopener">“Strangler Bob” read by Dermot Mulroney</a> sounds all brawny and seen-it-all. <a href="https://soundcloud.com/penguin-audio/the-largesse-of-the-sea" rel="nofollow noopener"> Liev Schreiber reads the last story</a> in the collection, “Dopplegänger, Poltergeist.” <br /><br />The Audible edition of the complete audiobook also has all five actors reading one each of five different stories. But be careful with this listen: Denis Johnson’s work is so unflashy, its skill can be easily overlooked when someone reads it aloud just right. The book is small and easy to carry: it allows one to appreciate the shortness of the stories, the way he laid it all out and wrote it down, and what he didn’t say. <br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15472459147316512193" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781787330634" href="/book/show/35657521-the-largesse-of-the-sea-maiden">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4617856-trudie?shelf=read-harder-2018">read-harder-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4617856-trudie?shelf=short-stories-novella">short-stories-novella</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2421484793" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8720744982672228024">* 2.5 *<br/><br/>Hmmmm... I don't know if I need to belabour the point too much but I simply didn't connect with this short story collection. <br/><br/>Objectively, I can see this is quite "literary" and original writing. There are great lines of dialog and a thoughtful contemplation of mortality. For me it was like a literary version of jazz, all weird dissonances and long improvised rifts seemingly taking us miles from the original path. I might have enjoyed that eclectic style more if I could have rustled up som</span>
  <span id="freeText8720744982672228024" style="display:none">* 2.5 *<br /><br />Hmmmm... I don't know if I need to belabour the point too much but I simply didn't connect with this short story collection. <br /><br />Objectively, I can see this is quite "literary" and original writing. There are great lines of dialog and a thoughtful contemplation of mortality. For me it was like a literary version of jazz, all weird dissonances and long improvised rifts seemingly taking us miles from the original path. I might have enjoyed that eclectic style more if I could have rustled up some bond with just one of Johnson's characters but curiously I felt quite removed from them all. <br /><br />I don't think I was the perfect reader for this book. I am doing this author an enormous disservice to pigeonhole this as "older white American male writing" as if that is a derogatory term, which I do not intend. It just simply indicates a point of reference from which I felt a little alienated. For whatever nebulous reasons there was a raft of indifference between me and the writing. I know this does no service whatsoever to a well respected and immensely talented author but unfortunately it was a feeling I could just not shake. <br /><br />Never mind ... onwards. </span>

<div id="review_2252289398" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2252289398" />
    <a title="Lee Klein " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/90786-lee-klein"><img alt="Lee Klein " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594811741p2/90786.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2252289398?book_show_action=true">Jan 10, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee Klein " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee Klein " href="/user/show/90786-lee-klein">Lee Klein </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2252289398" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3916559427665680182">Set in Bodoni Bold, the same old familiar typeface as <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/608287.Jesus__Son' title='Jesus' Son by Denis Johnson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jesus' Son</a>, this collection of five longish stories feels like the career-ending bookend that it is. He wrote these stories while alive but now he's not -- the second-to-last story, an autofictional one about a writer friend dying on a hundred-acre place out somewhere south of Austin, not tying-up his robe, seeing ghosts, ends perfectly like this: "The world keeps turning. It's plain to you that at the time I write this, I'm not dead. But may</span>
  <span id="freeText3916559427665680182" style="display:none">Set in Bodoni Bold, the same old familiar typeface as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/608287.Jesus__Son" title="Jesus' Son by Denis Johnson" rel="nofollow noopener">Jesus' Son</a>, this collection of five longish stories feels like the career-ending bookend that it is. He wrote these stories while alive but now he's not -- the second-to-last story, an autofictional one about a writer friend dying on a hundred-acre place out somewhere south of Austin, not tying-up his robe, seeing ghosts, ends perfectly like this: "The world keeps turning. It's plain to you that at the time I write this, I'm not dead. But maybe by the time you read this." Each of these five stories seemed like an improvement on the last, deepened my engagement and appreciation of his sensibility and approach (at one point he says "Writing. It's easy work . . . Whatever happens to you, you put it on the page, work it into a shape, cast it in a light"). They're all first-person but the fourth and fifth wear a thinner mask than the first three. The first one, the title story, about an advertisement copywriter, I should read again. I can hardly remember it. The second epistolary story, too. I wasn't into them as I was into the others, in part because they seemed like put-ons, that is, that the author was wearing the voice. But after a few pages into the third story "Strangler Bob" I felt the world and spirit of "Jesus' Son" alive and kicking again, that particularly Denis Johnson vitality apparent in the language. The stories are always about moving toward light in the midst of darkness and death, but more so there's a sense of life lived and not just simply caught but <i>raised-up</i> in language that feels real but never "energetic" or "voicey" but intoxicated (poisoned), elevated, unpredictable, casually incantatory, wise thanks to misfortune and folly and divine psychedelic intervention and insight. And it all comes together and takes off in service of a bit of a mystery as in the last story about the "real" poet whose real work involves following his obsession with a conspiracy involving Elvis's stillborn twin. I somehow haven't read his novels although I've read "Jesus' Son" thrice. I'll rectify that soon.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3916559427665680182" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2159742089" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2159742089" />
    <a title="Jill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2228181-jill"><img alt="Jill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507660688p2/2228181.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2159742089?book_show_action=true">Oct 26, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jill" href="/user/show/2228181-jill">Jill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/36117145-the-largesse-of-the-sea-maiden">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2159742089" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4803026617272752011">What an amazing gift it must be to take instances of everyday life and infuse them with lyricism and meaning.<br/><br/>Denis Johnson, in this posthumous collection, expands on this head-on in a story that is appropriately titled, “Triumph Over The Grave.” His narrator confides, ‘Writing. It is easy work…Whatever happens to you, you put it on a page, work it into a shape, cast it in a light.”<br/><br/>For those who have ever tried their hand at writing—I count myself among them—it’s evident that writing is anything </span>
  <span id="freeText4803026617272752011" style="display:none">What an amazing gift it must be to take instances of everyday life and infuse them with lyricism and meaning.<br /><br />Denis Johnson, in this posthumous collection, expands on this head-on in a story that is appropriately titled, “Triumph Over The Grave.” His narrator confides, ‘Writing. It is easy work…Whatever happens to you, you put it on a page, work it into a shape, cast it in a light.”<br /><br />For those who have ever tried their hand at writing—I count myself among them—it’s evident that writing is anything BUT easy. And who knows whether it was for Denis Johnson? His writing would give testimony that it appears so.<br /><br />Death is not far from the thoughts of characters in thee stories, completed shortly before Johnson’s own demise from liver cancer this year. In one of my favorites, “The Spotlight on Idaho”, an alcoholic in rehab who is coping with the side effects of Antabuse, writes a series of revealing letters to everyone from siblings to his sponsor to Satan. In the process, he lays himself bare and reveals the forlornness and yearning of his character. “Hey God where is you you ain’t nowhere”, he writes poignantly.<br /><br />Another story is focused on an aging and damaged writer, whose own life – and that of two close friends – are all spiraling toward death. “The world keeps turning. It’s plain to you that at the time I write this I’m not dead. But maybe by the time you read this””, he tell shis audience.<br /><br />There’s a story titled Doppelganger, Poltergeist about a writing professor’s star student who is convinced that Elvis Presley’s stillborn twin never died and actually took his place after Elvis’ stint in the army. And then there’s the eponymous “The Largesse of the Sea Maiden”, composed of moments both big and small from the life of an ad man. <br /><br />All these damaged characters are at the edge, seeking meaning or at least, cohesion in their lives. Denis Johnson was an astounding writer and he leaves behind a last gift. I owe a debt of thanks to Random House for sending me an ARC in exchange for an honest review.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4803026617272752011" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2764719636" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2764719636" />
    <a title="Kathleen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/47188780-kathleen"><img alt="Kathleen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1520288810p2/47188780.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2764719636?book_show_action=true">Mar 26, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathleen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathleen" href="/user/show/47188780-kathleen">Kathleen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2764719636" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15638072371801330779">National Book Critics Circle Longlist 2018. Johnson writes about human mortality in each of his five short stories. His own mortality may have been weighing heavily on his mind as he was suffering from liver cancer. One of his characters states—“It’s plain to you that at the time I wrote this, I’m not dead, but maybe by the time you read it.” In ‘Triumph Over the Grave’, Johnson seems to be reminiscing about his writing career--“Whatever happens to you, you put it on the page, work it into a sha</span>
  <span id="freeText15638072371801330779" style="display:none">National Book Critics Circle Longlist 2018. Johnson writes about human mortality in each of his five short stories. His own mortality may have been weighing heavily on his mind as he was suffering from liver cancer. One of his characters states—“It’s plain to you that at the time I wrote this, I’m not dead, but maybe by the time you read it.” In ‘Triumph Over the Grave’, Johnson seems to be reminiscing about his writing career--“Whatever happens to you, you put it on the page, work it into a shape, cast it in a light.” Enjoy these excellently written short stories.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15638072371801330779" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2275510940" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2275510940" />
    <a title="Doug" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25667717-doug"><img alt="Doug" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605117426p2/25667717.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2275510940?book_show_action=true">May 10, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Doug" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Doug" href="/user/show/25667717-doug">Doug</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2275510940" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5541270200203411760">2.5, rounded up.<br/><br/>First off, let me preface things by reiterating that short stories, those amuse-gueule of the book realm, are not really my forte. And that the only other Johnson I've read is his OTHER highly acclaimed collection of stories, 'Jesus' Son', which I also didn't particularly appreciate. So sadly, I must just state that Johnson and I are not ever going to be a good match, and I just don't 'get' what others see in his rather prosaic tales of the downbeat and derelict - although I am p</span>
  <span id="freeText5541270200203411760" style="display:none">2.5, rounded up.<br /><br />First off, let me preface things by reiterating that short stories, those amuse-gueule of the book realm, are not really my forte. And that the only other Johnson I've read is his OTHER highly acclaimed collection of stories, 'Jesus' Son', which I also didn't particularly appreciate. So sadly, I must just state that Johnson and I are not ever going to be a good match, and I just don't 'get' what others see in his rather prosaic tales of the downbeat and derelict - although I am prepared to state it is quite possibly a case of literary 'It's not YOU, it's me'. <br /><br />To be fair, there are some poetic stylizations that land, but I just never feel very connected or involved with any of his rambling plotlines. The two stories about alcoholics ('The Starlight on Idaho') and jailbirds ('Strangler Bob'), seem tired and done to death. The final story ('Doppelgänger, Poltergeist') is a messy conflation of bizarre Elvis conspiracy theories and 9/11. 'Triumph Over the Grave' tells the sad stories of two failed writers approaching death, which at least has a lovely elegiac quality to it, given Johnson's own recent demise. Perhaps the most successful is the titular tale, although it mainly seems like a series of random vignettes that make as little concrete sense as its title. <br /><br /> Perhaps his novels would fare better with me, but at this point, I doubt I'll ever pick one up. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5541270200203411760" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dax" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dax" href="/user/show/72907701-dax">Dax</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/72907701-dax?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/72907701-dax?shelf=top-ten-2018">top-ten-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/72907701-dax?shelf=5-stars">5-stars</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2208261977" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4063214526773231050">Johnson was a poet first and foremost, and it shows in his fiction writing, particularly so in this impressive collection of five short stories. Clearly, Johnson imagined life as an attempt to navigate the darkness and pitfalls that are inevitably encountered on one's path through this world. This theme is the connective tissue that binds these five stories together, and coupled with Johnson's talent for imagery and perfectly crafted sentences, I was left shaking my head in wonder and jealousy. </span>
  <span id="freeText4063214526773231050" style="display:none">Johnson was a poet first and foremost, and it shows in his fiction writing, particularly so in this impressive collection of five short stories. Clearly, Johnson imagined life as an attempt to navigate the darkness and pitfalls that are inevitably encountered on one's path through this world. This theme is the connective tissue that binds these five stories together, and coupled with Johnson's talent for imagery and perfectly crafted sentences, I was left shaking my head in wonder and jealousy. <br /><br />The title story and "Triumph Over the Grave" are personal favorites. I know of no other writers who can illustrate the mind's capacity to wander tangentially quite like Johnson. Delillo maybe, but he's a distant second.<br /><br />The final lines of "Triumph Over the Grave" sent a few chills up my spine:<br /><br />"The world keeps turning. It's plain to you that at the time I write this, I'm not dead. But maybe by the time you read it."<br /><br />Wish DJ hadn't left us so soon.</span>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2295772847" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2295772847" />
    <a title="Krista" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4729639-krista"><img alt="Krista" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1579967825p2/4729639.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2295772847?book_show_action=true">Feb 14, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Krista" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Krista" href="/user/show/4729639-krista">Krista</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4729639-krista?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4729639-krista?shelf=2018">2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2295772847" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10632894327951405243"><blockquote><b>Three Rules To Write By</b><br/><br/>Write naked. That means to write what you would never say.<br/><br/>Write in blood. As if ink is so precious you can’t waste it.<br/><br/>Write in exile, as if you are never going to get home again, and you have to call back every detail.<br/><br/><b>Denis Johnson</b></blockquote><br/>Having died in 2017 of liver cancer, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35135343.The_Largesse_of_the_Sea_Maiden_Stories' title='The Largesse of the Sea Maiden Stories by Denis Johnson' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Largesse of the Sea Maiden</a> will be Denis Johnson's final release; a collection of five short stories, two of which were not previously published elsewhere. I loved this collection, but am having a hard tim</span>
  <span id="freeText10632894327951405243" style="display:none"><blockquote><b>Three Rules To Write By</b><br /><br />Write naked. That means to write what you would never say.<br /><br />Write in blood. As if ink is so precious you can’t waste it.<br /><br />Write in exile, as if you are never going to get home again, and you have to call back every detail.<br /><br /><b>Denis Johnson</b></blockquote><br />Having died in 2017 of liver cancer, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35135343.The_Largesse_of_the_Sea_Maiden_Stories" title="The Largesse of the Sea Maiden Stories by Denis Johnson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Largesse of the Sea Maiden</a> will be Denis Johnson's final release; a collection of five short stories, two of which were not previously published elsewhere. I loved this collection, but am having a hard time articulating why: Johnson's magic is certainly to be found in the words he uses – when poets also excel at prose, the results can be sublime – but his real expertise is in the pivots and transitions between the lines that suddenly illuminate a story like a spotlight from above. And to give a sense of what I mean by that, I'd need to excerpt an entire story – which, of course, I won't do – so you'll just need to pick this up and see for yourself.<br /><br /><i>
  <blockquote>I wonder if you're like me, if you collect and squirrel away in your soul certain odd moments when the Mystery winks at you.</blockquote>
</i><br />This line seems to capture the link between these stories – they could all be called expositions of the moments when the Mystery winks at you; not necessarily all uncanny experiences, but those times when you glimpse behind the curtain nonetheless. Watching a grown woman cry as she's dared to kiss an amputee's leg stump; an inmate climbs and swings simian-like across the outside bars of unlocked cells; a man confesses to digging up a generations-old infant's grave in the dead of night: these are strange tales told matter-of-factly. These stories are full of death – suicide, lethal injection, the sudden heart attack and the drawn out cancer battle – and filled with the ways we separate ourselves from one another: the series of locked gates you must pass through to reach a remote ranch house; the obscene offer handed under the bathroom stall's partition; the death row visitor's room and the peepshow booth, both with telephone handsets and a wall of safety glass preventing skin-on-skin contact. There are throughlines here that seem to reveal what Johnson himself was contemplating in his final months.<br /><br /><i>
  <blockquote>I note that I've lived longer in the past, now, than I can expect to live in the future. I have more to remember than I have to look forward to. Memory fades, not much of the past stays, and I wouldn't mind forgetting a lot more of it. </blockquote>
</i><br />As for the particulars, this collection contains the title story, <u>The Largesse of the Sea Maiden</u>, written from the POV of an “ad man”, it's a collection of odd stories from his life and career (just a lovely piece); <u>The Starlight on Idaho</u> is a series of letters written by a man in rehab who just might be going crazy from the Antabuse he's prescribed (this one was my favourite; it was unbelievable to me how a whole life was revealed through these ever-more-erratic ramblings); <u>Strangler Bob</u> is written by a man in a county lockup, surrounded by a variety of unusual characters (this was my least favourite, but as it might be a revisit to Johnson's most beloved character from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/608287.Jesus__Son" title="Jesus' Son by Denis Johnson" rel="nofollow noopener">Jesus' Son</a> – which I have yet to read – it could be more meaningful to other readers); <u>Triumph Over the Grave</u>, wherein a writer recounts a story from the past and one from the present in which friends of his die (which is so self-aware as Johnson himself was dying that its final line left me stricken); and <u>Doppelgänger, Poltergeist</u>, from the POV of a poetry professor who mentors a more talented younger poet and becomes his confessor for mad deeds and beliefs. <br /><br /><i>
  <blockquote>The Past just left. Its remnants, I claim, are mostly fiction.</blockquote>
</i><br />Because so many of the narrators of these stories are writers themselves, it usually felt like Johnson himself was speaking to me. And because so many of these stories were about end-of-life experiences, and the unreliability of memory, and the writer's efforts to take lived experience and “put it on a page, work it into a shape, cast it in a light”, it <i>felt</i> personal and long-considered. This is masterful work and a fitting cap to a celebrated career.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10632894327951405243" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Katie Long" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Katie Long" href="/user/show/46311769-katie-long">Katie Long</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10109026641768294755">This is my first Denis Johnson and I’m guessing this may not be the best place to start. There is some undeniably gorgeous writing here, but I had a hard time caring about any of it. Somehow it felt as though there was a wall between these characters and me.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2274753770" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2274753770" />
    <a title="Truman32" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22271857-truman32"><img alt="Truman32" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1375371520p2/22271857.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2274753770?book_show_action=true">Feb 03, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Truman32" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Truman32" href="/user/show/22271857-truman32">Truman32</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2274753770" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9057616119962252049">I first became aware of Denis Johnson from his seminal short story collection, <b>Jesus’ Son</b>; his National Book Award winning novel, <b>Tree of Smoke</b>; as well as his perfectly timed (and downright nasty) passes to Larry Bird that eventually brought the championship banner back to the Boston Garden after the 1984 Finals. <br/><br/><b>The Largesse of the Sea Maiden</b>, published posthumously, is Johnson’s return to short stories and it is sensational. If you are going to read this gem of a book I would suggest you inve</span>
  <span id="freeText9057616119962252049" style="display:none">I first became aware of Denis Johnson from his seminal short story collection, <b>Jesus’ Son</b>; his National Book Award winning novel, <b>Tree of Smoke</b>; as well as his perfectly timed (and downright nasty) passes to Larry Bird that eventually brought the championship banner back to the Boston Garden after the 1984 Finals. <br /><br /><b>The Largesse of the Sea Maiden</b>, published posthumously, is Johnson’s return to short stories and it is sensational. If you are going to read this gem of a book I would suggest you invest in a pair of sock garters (an actual thing!) because this baby is going to knock your socks off! And it would be shame if one of your knocked off socks were to fly into the face of the MBTA bus driver just trying to get you to your stop, momentarily blinding him. The next thing you know 40,000 screaming pounds of city bus is careening down Commonwealth Avenue at a breakneck pace and slamming into one of those Boston Duck tour vehicles (is it a boat? Is it a bus? Why is everyone quacking?!!). Abruptly everything erupts into a flaming Hell of twisted metal, shrieking tourists, melting faces, and blazing stockings… <br /><br />While the tales in Johnson’s first (and only other) book of short fiction,<b> Jesus’ Son</b> (published way way back in 1992) dealt entirely with young adults (usually junkies and failures, lost and adrift, soon to hit rock bottom); in <b>The Largesse of the Sea Maiden</b> we are given narratives of older adults (still rundown and damaged) but this time dealing with the dissolutions of marriages, the death of friends and family, and the inability to understand their relevance in the world. Death overshadows almost every word in this book perhaps because Johnson was facing his own mortality enduring a losing battle with liver cancer. He died in May of last year. But if Kafka was correct in saying that, “the meaning of life is that it stops”, then these stories hold so much meaning-- significance, and import. <br /><br />The best stories here are the first three: The Largesse of the Sea Maiden, The Starlight on Idaho, and Strangler Bob. But let’s be honest, every tale in this way too brief book—even the imperfect later two-could easily dance circles around most of the literature taking up space on the tables and bays of your nearest Barnes and Noble. It’s like criticizing ice cream. Yes, maybe you had some gelato by the Trevi fountain on your last vacation that was out-of-this-world, but we <i>are</i> still talking about ice cream here, folks. And ice cream is still better than 99% of the stuff you ever have or ever will stuff into your mouth. <br /><br />Johnson is a master of the understatement and he achieves so much with few words. His writing skips your brain, instead choosing to speak directly with your heart. Loved this sucker!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9057616119962252049" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2398747333" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2398747333" />
    <a title="Ken" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/730754-ken"><img alt="Ken" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565570004p2/730754.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2398747333?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ken" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ken" href="/user/show/730754-ken">Ken</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/730754-ken?shelf=finished-in-2018">finished-in-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/730754-ken?shelf=short-story-collection">short-story-collection</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2398747333" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15835226412679979004">Five stories, the final ones, from a great talent, Denis Johnson. Yes, I preferred <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/608287.Jesus__Son' title='Jesus' Son by Denis Johnson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jesus' Son</a>, but that's the proverbial "tough act you'll never follow," as they say of runaway successes.<br/><br/>In this collection, I liked "Triumph Over the Grave" (#4) best. The middle prison story, "Strangler Bob," did little for me, and the topic of the last, "Doppelgänger, Poltergeist" is not my favorite -- Elvis Presley -- so I was a lost lamb to begin with.<br/><br/>That said, glad I read it, and sad to see Johnson go.</span>
  <span id="freeText15835226412679979004" style="display:none">Five stories, the final ones, from a great talent, Denis Johnson. Yes, I preferred <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/608287.Jesus__Son" title="Jesus' Son by Denis Johnson" rel="nofollow noopener">Jesus' Son</a>, but that's the proverbial "tough act you'll never follow," as they say of runaway successes.<br /><br />In this collection, I liked "Triumph Over the Grave" (#4) best. The middle prison story, "Strangler Bob," did little for me, and the topic of the last, "Doppelgänger, Poltergeist" is not my favorite -- Elvis Presley -- so I was a lost lamb to begin with.<br /><br />That said, glad I read it, and sad to see Johnson go.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15835226412679979004" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

			<a class="actionLink" href="/topic/show/19613586-combine-editions---the-largesse-of-the-sea-maiden-by-denis-johnson">Combine Editions - The Largesse of the Sea Maiden by Denis Johnson</a>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ONSp5pvrmWVJzPIB1NL5+yh/Dc6xh7++wq3DLbfJxPwC8sA0ILVQDe7nRmAjJ/Mk/iDqBn8KMdmZ04Wceoo2Zg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('sPF8927HU8MmUFx4609mo4DPQGrhID4L5ORoRMmZZPWK1xUl1Zmaq4F76Bkcumx8VpCnoi+tsGy/mi71BNqWbw==')})
  }

The first section, Folly tells the story of Alice, a young American editor, and her relationship with the famous and much older writer Ezra Blazer. A tender and exquisite account of an unexpected romance that takes place in New York during the early years of the Iraq War, Folly also suggests an aspiring novelist’s coming-of-age. By contrast, Madness is narrated by Amar, an Iraqi-American man who, on his way to visit his brother in Kurdistan, is detained by immigration officers and spends the last weekend of 2008 in a holding room in Heathrow. These two seemingly disparate stories gain resonance as their perspectives interact and overlap, with yet new implications for their relationship revealed in an unexpected coda.A stunning debut from a rising literary star, Asymmetry is an urgent, important, and truly original work that will captivate any reader while also posing arresting questions about the very nature of fiction itself.<\/span>\n <span id="freeTextContainer7407334680305633154">Poet, playwright and author Denis Johnson was born in Munich, West Germany, in 1949 and was raised in Tokyo, Manila and Washington. He earned a masters' degree from the University of Iowa and received many awards for his work, including a Lannan Fellowship in Fiction (1993), a Whiting Writer's Award (1986), the Aga Khan Prize for Fiction from the Paris Review for Train Dreams, and most recently, t</span>
  <span id="freeText7407334680305633154" style="display:none">Poet, playwright and author Denis Johnson was born in Munich, West Germany, in 1949 and was raised in Tokyo, Manila and Washington. He earned a masters' degree from the University of Iowa and received many awards for his work, including a Lannan Fellowship in Fiction (1993), a Whiting Writer's Award (1986), the Aga Khan Prize for Fiction from the Paris Review for Train Dreams, and most recently, the National Book Award for Fiction (2007).<br /></span>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/57703470-the-largesse-of-the-sea-maiden">Quotes from The Largesse of t...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;After a noticeable silence, he'd recently published a book of technically baffling poems, with line breaks so arbitrary and frequent as to be useless, arrhythmic. On the page they look like some of Charles Bukowski's skinny, chatty, muttering-stuttering antiverses. Impossibly, Mark's words make music, the faraway strains of an irresistible jazz. It's plain to any reader, within a few lines—well, go read the poems and see, Marcus Ahearn traffics with the ineffable. He makes the mind of the speaker present, in that here-and-now where the reader actually reads—that place. Such a rare thing. Samuel Beckett. Jean Follain, Ionesco—the composer Billy Strayhorn. Mark calls his process "psychic improvisation" and referred me to the painter Paul Klee; the term was Klee's. "You just get out a pen and a notebook and let your mind go long," he told me.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/9570391-after-a-noticeable-silence-he-d-recently-published-a-book-of">15 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;The Past just left. Its remnants, I claim, are mostly fiction. We're stranded here with the threadbare patchwork of memory, you with yours, I with mine.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/9672529-the-past-just-left-its-remnants-i-claim-are-mostly">14 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
        <a class="actionLink" style="float: right" href="/work/quotes/57703470-the-largesse-of-the-sea-maiden">More quotes&hellip;</a>
